<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Liver</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Vertebrate organ involved in metabolism</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the organ. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Liver_(disambiguation)" title="Liver (disambiguation)">Liver (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q9368" title="d:Q9368">edit on Wikidata</a>]</small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>liver</b> is a major  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organ</a> only found in vertebrates which performs many essential biological functions such as <a href="/wiki/Detoxification" title="Detoxification">detoxification</a> of the organism, and the <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">synthesis of proteins</a> and biochemicals necessary for <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a> and growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancer.ca_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-cancer.ca-4">[4]</a></sup> In humans, it is located in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadrant_(anatomy)" title="Quadrant (anatomy)">right upper quadrant</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a>, below the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a>. Its other roles in <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> include the regulation of <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen storage</a>, decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, and the production of <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancer.ca_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-cancer.ca-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The liver is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accessory_digestive_organ" title="Accessory digestive organ">accessory digestive organ</a> that produces <a href="/wiki/Bile" title="Bile">bile</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkaline" title="Alkaline">alkaline</a> fluid containing <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bile_acid" title="Bile acid">bile acids</a>,  which helps the <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_degradation" title="Fatty acid degradation">breakdown of fat</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder" title="Gallbladder">gallbladder</a>, a small pouch that sits just under the liver, stores bile produced by the liver which is afterwards moved to the small intestine to complete digestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tortora945_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tortora945-5">[5]</a></sup> The liver's highly specialized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_tissue" title="Biological tissue">tissue</a>, consisting mostly of <a href="/wiki/Hepatocyte" title="Hepatocyte">hepatocytes</a>, regulates a wide variety of high-volume biochemical reactions, including the synthesis and breakdown of small and complex molecules, many of which are necessary for normal vital functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Estimates regarding the organ's total number of functions vary, but is generally cited as being around 500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is not known how to compensate for the absence of liver function in the long term, although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_dialysis" title="Liver dialysis">liver dialysis</a> techniques can be used in the short term. Artificial livers have not been developed to promote long-term replacement in the absence of the liver. As of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMedicine1900159_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMedicine1900159-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liver_transplantation" title="Liver transplantation">liver transplantation</a> is the only option for complete <a href="/wiki/Liver_failure" title="Liver failure">liver failure</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Other_animals"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Other animals</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Works_cited"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Works cited</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gray1085.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Gray1085.png/240px-Gray1085.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Gray1085.png/360px-Gray1085.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Gray1085.png/480px-Gray1085.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gray1085.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The liver, viewed from above, showing the left and right lobes separated by the <a href="/wiki/Falciform_ligament" title="Falciform ligament">falciform ligament</a></div></div></div>
<p>The liver is a reddish-brown, wedge-shaped <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organ</a> with two
<a href="/wiki/Lobe_(anatomy)" title="Lobe (anatomy)">lobes</a> of unequal size and shape. A human liver normally weighs approximately 1.5 kg (3.3 lb)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robbins_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-robbins-9">[9]</a></sup> and has a width of about 15 cm (6 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> There is considerable size variation between individuals, with the standard <a href="/wiki/Reference_range" title="Reference range">reference range</a> for men being 970–1,860 g (2.14–4.10 lb)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolinaDiMaio2012_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-MolinaDiMaio2012-11">[11]</a></sup> and for women 600–1,770 g (1.32–3.90 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolinaDiMaio2015_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-MolinaDiMaio2015-12">[12]</a></sup> It is both the heaviest internal organ and the largest <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">gland</a> in the human body.
Located in the right upper quadrant of the <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_cavity" title="Abdominal cavity">abdominal cavity</a>, it rests just below the diaphragm, to the right of the stomach and overlies the <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder" title="Gallbladder">gallbladder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tortora945_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tortora945-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The liver is connected to two large <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a>: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatic_artery" title="Hepatic artery">hepatic artery</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Portal_vein" title="Portal vein">portal vein</a>. The hepatic artery carries oxygen-rich blood from the aorta via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celiac_trunk" title="Celiac trunk">celiac trunk</a>, whereas the portal vein carries blood rich in digested nutrients from the entire <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a> and also from the <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMedicine1900159_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-EMedicine1900159-8">[8]</a></sup> These blood vessels subdivide into small capillaries known as <a href="/wiki/Liver_sinusoid" title="Liver sinusoid">liver sinusoids</a>, which then lead to <a href="/wiki/Lobules_of_liver" title="Lobules of liver">lobules</a>.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="liver_capsule"></span>Lobules are the functional units of the liver. Each lobule is made up of millions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatic_cells" title="Hepatic cells">hepatic cells</a> (hepatocytes), which are the basic metabolic cells. The lobules are held together by a fine, dense, irregular, <a class="external text" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fibroelastic">fibroelastic</a> connective tissue layer extending from the fibrous capsule covering the entire liver known as <a href="/wiki/Francis_Glisson" title="Francis Glisson">Glisson's capsule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancer.ca_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-cancer.ca-4">[4]</a></sup> This extends into the structure of the liver by accompanying the blood vessels, ducts, and nerves at the hepatic hilum. The whole surface of the liver, except for the <a href="/wiki/Bare_area_of_the_liver" title="Bare area of the liver">bare area</a>, is covered in a <a href="/wiki/Serous_membrane" title="Serous membrane">serous</a> coat derived from the <a href="/wiki/Peritoneum" title="Peritoneum">peritoneum</a>, and this firmly adheres to the inner <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glisson%27s_capsule" title="Glisson's capsule">Glisson's capsule</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gross_anatomy">Gross anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Gross anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Terminology related to the liver often starts in <a href="/wiki/List_of_medical_roots,_suffixes_and_prefixes#H" title="List of medical roots, suffixes and prefixes"><i>hepat-</i></a> from ἡπατο-, from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word for liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Etymology_online_hepatic_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Etymology_online_hepatic-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lobes">Lobes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Lobes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lobes_of_liver" title="Lobes of liver">Lobes of liver</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sobo_1906_389.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1356" data-file-width="1895" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Sobo_1906_389.png/240px-Sobo_1906_389.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Sobo_1906_389.png/360px-Sobo_1906_389.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Sobo_1906_389.png/480px-Sobo_1906_389.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sobo_1906_389.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The liver, viewed from below, surface showing four lobes and the impressions</div></div></div>
<p>The liver is <a href="/wiki/Gross_anatomy" title="Gross anatomy">grossly</a> divided into two parts when viewed from above – a right and a left lobe - and four parts when viewed from below (left, right, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caudate_lobe" title="Caudate lobe">caudate</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadrate_lobe" title="Quadrate lobe">quadrate lobes</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Falciform_ligament" title="Falciform ligament">falciform ligament</a> makes a superficial division of the liver into a left and right lobe. From below, the two additional lobes are located between the right and left lobes, one in front of the other. A line can be imagined running from the left of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vena_cava" title="Vena cava">vena cava</a> and all the way forward to divide the liver and gallbladder into two halves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> This line is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cantlie%27s_line" title="Cantlie's line">Cantlie's line</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other anatomical landmarks include the <a href="/wiki/Ligamentum_venosum" title="Ligamentum venosum">ligamentum venosum</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Round_ligament_of_the_liver" title="Round ligament of the liver">round ligament of the liver</a>, which further divide the left side of the liver in two sections. An important anatomical landmark, the <a href="/wiki/Porta_hepatis" title="Porta hepatis">porta hepatis</a>, divides this left portion into four segments, which can be numbered starting at the caudate lobe as I in an anticlockwise manner. From this parietal view, seven segments can be seen, because the eighth segment is only visible in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">visceral</a> view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surfaces">Surfaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Surfaces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On the diaphragmatic surface, apart from a triangular bare area where it connects to the diaphragm, the liver is covered by a thin, double-layered membrane, the <a href="/wiki/Peritoneum" title="Peritoneum">peritoneum</a>, that helps to reduce friction against other organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-18">[18]</a></sup> This surface covers the convex shape of the two lobes where it accommodates the shape of the diaphragm. The peritoneum folds back on itself to form the <a href="/wiki/Falciform_ligament" title="Falciform ligament">falciform ligament</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Right_triangular_ligament" title="Right triangular ligament">right</a> and <a href="/wiki/Left_triangular_ligament" title="Left triangular ligament">left triangular ligaments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>These <a href="/wiki/Peritoneal_ligament" title="Peritoneal ligament">peritoneal ligaments</a> are not related to the anatomic ligaments in joints, and the right and left triangular ligaments have no known functional importance, though they serve as surface landmarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-19">[19]</a></sup> The falciform ligament functions to attach the liver to the <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">posterior</a> portion of the <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">anterior</a> body wall.
</p><p>The visceral surface or inferior surface is uneven and concave. It is covered in peritoneum apart from where it attaches the gallbladder and the porta hepatis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-18">[18]</a></sup> The fossa of gall bladder lies to the right of the quadrate lobe, occupied by the gallbladder with its cystic duct close to the right end of porta hepatis.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impressions">Impressions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Impressions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gray1087-liver.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Gray1087-liver.png/280px-Gray1087-liver.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Gray1087-liver.png/420px-Gray1087-liver.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Gray1087-liver.png/560px-Gray1087-liver.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gray1087-liver.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Impressions of the liver</div></div></div>
<p>Several impressions on the surface of the liver accommodate the various adjacent structures and organs. Underneath the right lobe and to the right of the gallbladder fossa are two impressions, one behind the other and separated by a ridge. The one in front is a shallow colic impression, formed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatic_flexure" title="Hepatic flexure">hepatic flexure</a> and the one behind is a deeper renal impression accommodating part of the right kidney and part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suprarenal_gland" title="Suprarenal gland">suprarenal gland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The suprarenal impression is a small, triangular, depressed area on the liver. It is located close to the right of the <a href="/wiki/Fossa_(anatomy)" title="Fossa (anatomy)">fossa</a>, between the bare area and the caudate lobe, and immediately above the renal impression. The greater part of the suprarenal impression is devoid of peritoneum and it lodges the right suprarenal gland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Dorland's_illustrated_medical_dictionary''2012925_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Dorland's_illustrated_medical_dictionary''2012925-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Medial to the renal impression is a third and slightly marked impression, lying between it and the neck of the gall bladder. This is caused by the descending portion of the duodenum, and is known as the duodenal impression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Dorland's_illustrated_medical_dictionary''2012925_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Dorland's_illustrated_medical_dictionary''2012925-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The inferior surface of the left lobe of the liver presents behind and to the left of the gastric impression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Dorland's_illustrated_medical_dictionary''2012925_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Dorland's_illustrated_medical_dictionary''2012925-21">[21]</a></sup> This is moulded over the upper front surface of the stomach, and to the right of this is a rounded eminence, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuber_omentale" title="Tuber omentale">tuber omentale</a>, which fits into the concavity of the lesser curvature of the stomach and lies in front of the anterior layer of the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_omentum" title="Lesser omentum">lesser omentum</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microscopic_anatomy">Microscopic anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Microscopic anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hepatic_structure2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="271" data-file-width="601" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Hepatic_structure2.svg/220px-Hepatic_structure2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Hepatic_structure2.svg/330px-Hepatic_structure2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Hepatic_structure2.svg/440px-Hepatic_structure2.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hepatic_structure2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cells, ducts, and blood vessels</div></div></div>
<p>Microscopically, each liver lobe is seen to be made up of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatic_lobule" title="Hepatic lobule">hepatic lobules</a>. The lobules are roughly hexagonal, and consist of plates of hepatocytes, and sinusoids radiating from a central vein towards an imaginary perimeter of interlobular portal triads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MooreK_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-MooreK-22">[22]</a></sup> The central vein joins to the hepatic vein to carry blood out from the liver. A distinctive component of a lobule is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portal_triad" title="Portal triad">portal triad</a>, which can be found running along each of the lobule's corners. The portal triad consists of the hepatic artery, the portal vein, and the common bile duct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-23">[23]</a></sup> The triad may be seen on a liver ultrasound, as a <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse_sign" title="Mickey Mouse sign">Mickey Mouse sign</a> with the portal vein as the head, and the hepatic artery, and the common bile duct as the ears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-med.emory_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-med.emory-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">Histology</a>, the study of microscopic anatomy, shows two major types of liver cell: <a href="/wiki/Parenchyma#Liver_parenchyma" title="Parenchyma">parenchymal cells</a> and nonparenchymal cells. About 70–85% of the liver volume is occupied by parenchymal hepatocytes. Nonparenchymal cells constitute 40% of the total number of liver cells but only 6.5% of its volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kmieć_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kmieć-25">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Liver_sinusoid" title="Liver sinusoid">liver sinusoids</a> are lined with two types of cell, <a href="/wiki/Liver_sinusoidal_endothelial_cell" title="Liver sinusoidal endothelial cell">sinusoidal endothelial cells</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phagocyte" title="Phagocyte">phagocytic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kupffer_cells" title="Kupffer cells">Kupffer cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hepatic_stellate_cell" title="Hepatic stellate cell">Hepatic stellate cells</a> are nonparenchymal cells found in the <a href="/wiki/Perisinusoidal_space" title="Perisinusoidal space">perisinusoidal space</a>, between a sinusoid and a hepatocyte.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kmieć_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kmieć-25">[25]</a></sup>
Additionally, intrahepatic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lymphocytes" title="Lymphocytes">lymphocytes</a> are often present in the sinusoidal lumen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kmieć_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kmieć-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Functional_anatomy">Functional anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Functional anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hilum_of_the_liver.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="465" data-file-width="675" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Hilum_of_the_liver.png/220px-Hilum_of_the_liver.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Hilum_of_the_liver.png/330px-Hilum_of_the_liver.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Hilum_of_the_liver.png/440px-Hilum_of_the_liver.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hilum_of_the_liver.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hilum of the liver, circled in yellow</div></div></div>
<p>The central area or <a href="/wiki/Hilum_(anatomy)" title="Hilum (anatomy)">hepatic hilum</a>, includes the opening known as the porta hepatis which carries the <a href="/wiki/Common_bile_duct" title="Common bile duct">common bile duct</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_hepatic_artery" title="Common hepatic artery">common hepatic artery</a>, and the opening for the portal vein. The duct, vein, and artery divide into left and right branches, and the areas of the liver supplied by these branches constitute the functional left and right lobes. The functional lobes are separated by the imaginary plane, Cantlie's line, joining the gallbladder fossa to the inferior vena cava. The plane separates the liver into the true right and left lobes. The middle hepatic vein also demarcates the true right and left lobes. The right lobe is further divided into an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior" title="Anterior">anterior</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posterior_(anatomy)" title="Posterior (anatomy)">posterior</a> segment by the right hepatic vein. The left lobe is divided into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medial_(anatomy)" title="Medial (anatomy)">medial</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antero-posteriorly" title="Antero-posteriorly">lateral</a> segments by the left hepatic vein.
</p><p>The hilum of the liver is described in terms of three <i>plates</i> that contain the <a href="/wiki/Bile_duct" title="Bile duct">bile ducts</a> and blood vessels. The contents of the whole plate system are surrounded by a sheath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kawarada_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kawarada-27">[27]</a></sup> The three plates are the <i>hilar plate</i>, the <i>cystic plate</i> and the <i>umbilical plate</i> and the plate system is the site of the many anatomical variations to be found in the liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kawarada_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kawarada-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Couinaud_classification_system">Couinaud classification system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Couinaud classification system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liver_segment" title="Liver segment">Liver segment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liver_04_Couinaud_classification_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Liver_04_Couinaud_classification_animation.gif/220px-Liver_04_Couinaud_classification_animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Liver_04_Couinaud_classification_animation.gif/330px-Liver_04_Couinaud_classification_animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f1/Liver_04_Couinaud_classification_animation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liver_04_Couinaud_classification_animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shape of human liver in animation, with eight Couinaud segments labelled</div></div></div>
<p>In the widely used <a href="/wiki/Claude_Couinaud" title="Claude Couinaud">Couinaud</a> system, the functional lobes are further divided into a total of eight subsegments based on a transverse plane through the bifurcation of the main portal vein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-radiopaedia.org_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-radiopaedia.org-28">[28]</a></sup> The caudate lobe is a separate structure that receives blood flow from both the right- and left-sided vascular branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3d_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-3d-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strunk_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-strunk-30">[30]</a></sup> The Couinaud classification divides the liver into eight functionally independent <a href="/wiki/Liver_segment" title="Liver segment">liver segments</a>. Each segment has its own vascular inflow, outflow and biliary drainage. In the centre of each segment are branches of the portal vein, hepatic artery, and bile duct. In the periphery of each segment is vascular outflow through the hepatic veins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-radiologyassistant.nl_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-radiologyassistant.nl-31">[31]</a></sup> The classification system uses the vascular supply in the liver to separate the functional units (numbered I to VIII) with unit 1, the caudate lobe, receiving its supply from both the right and the left branches of the portal vein. It contains one or more hepatic veins which drain directly into the <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior vena cava</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-radiopaedia.org_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-radiopaedia.org-28">[28]</a></sup> The remainder of the units (II to VIII) are numbered in a clockwise fashion:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-radiologyassistant.nl_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-radiologyassistant.nl-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gene_and_protein_expression">Gene and protein expression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gene and protein expression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics#Gene_and_protein_expression" title="Bioinformatics">Bioinformatics § Gene and protein expression</a></div>
<p>About 20,000 protein coding genes are expressed in human cells and 60% of these genes are expressed in a normal, adult liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Over 400 genes are more specifically expressed in the liver, with some 150 genes highly specific for liver tissue. A large fraction of the corresponding liver-specific proteins are mainly expressed in hepatocytes and secreted into the blood and constitute plasma proteins and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatokines" title="Hepatokines">hepatokines</a>. Other liver-specific proteins are certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_enzymes" title="Liver enzymes">liver enzymes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyacid_oxidase_(glycolate_oxidase)_1" title="Hydroxyacid oxidase (glycolate oxidase) 1">HAO1</a> and <a href="/wiki/RDH16" title="RDH16">RDH16</a>, proteins involved in bile synthesis such as <a href="/wiki/BAAT" title="BAAT">BAAT</a> and <a href="/wiki/SLC27A5" title="SLC27A5">SLC27A5</a>, and transporter proteins involved in the metabolism of drugs, such as <a href="/wiki/ABCB11" title="ABCB11">ABCB11</a> and <a href="/wiki/GLUT2" title="GLUT2">SLC2A2</a>. Examples of highly liver-specific proteins include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/APOA2" title="APOA2">apolipoprotein A II</a>, coagulation factors <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prothrombin" title="Prothrombin">F2</a> and <a href="/wiki/Factor_IX" title="Factor IX">F9</a>, <a href="/wiki/Complement_system" title="Complement system">complement factor related proteins</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Fibrinogen_beta_chain" title="Fibrinogen beta chain">fibrinogen beta chain</a> protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CT_Scan_Thorax_Liver.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="895" data-file-width="917" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/CT_Scan_Thorax_Liver.jpg/220px-CT_Scan_Thorax_Liver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/CT_Scan_Thorax_Liver.jpg/330px-CT_Scan_Thorax_Liver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/CT_Scan_Thorax_Liver.jpg/440px-CT_Scan_Thorax_Liver.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CT_Scan_Thorax_Liver.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>CT scan showing an adult liver in the axial plane.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Organogenesis" title="Organogenesis">Organogenesis</a>, the development of the organs, takes place from the third to the eighth week during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_embryogenesis" title="Human embryogenesis">embryogenesis</a>. The origins of the liver lie in both the ventral portion of the <a href="/wiki/Foregut" title="Foregut">foregut</a> <a href="/wiki/Endoderm" title="Endoderm">endoderm</a> (endoderm being one of the three embryonic <a href="/wiki/Germ_layer" title="Germ layer">germ layers</a>) and the constituents of the adjacent <a href="/wiki/Septum_transversum" title="Septum transversum">septum transversum</a> <a href="/wiki/Mesenchyme" title="Mesenchyme">mesenchyme</a>. In the human <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hepatic_diverticulum" title="Hepatic diverticulum">hepatic diverticulum</a> is the tube of endoderm that extends out from the foregut into the surrounding mesenchyme. The mesenchyme of septum transversum induces this endoderm to proliferate, to branch, and to form the glandular epithelium of the liver. A portion of the hepatic diverticulum (that region closest to the digestive tube) continues to function as the <a href="/wiki/Biliary_tract" title="Biliary tract">drainage duct</a> of the liver, and a branch from this duct produces the gallbladder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Besides signals from the septum transversum mesenchyme, <a href="/wiki/Fibroblast_growth_factor" title="Fibroblast growth factor">fibroblast growth factor</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Heart_development" title="Heart development">developing heart</a> also contributes to hepatic competence, along with <a href="/wiki/Retinoic_acid" title="Retinoic acid">retinoic acid</a> emanating from the <a href="/wiki/Lateral_plate_mesoderm" title="Lateral plate mesoderm">lateral plate mesoderm</a>. The hepatic endodermal cells undergo a morphological transition from columnar to pseudostratified resulting in thickening into the early <a href="/wiki/Hepatic_diverticulum" title="Hepatic diverticulum">liver bud</a>. Their expansion forms a population of the bipotential hepatoblasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21337461_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21337461-36">[36]</a></sup> Hepatic stellate cells are derived from mesenchyme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>After migration of hepatoblasts into the septum transversum mesenchyme, the hepatic architecture begins to be established, with liver sinusoids and bile canaliculi appearing. The liver bud separates into the lobes. The left <a href="/wiki/Umbilical_vein" title="Umbilical vein">umbilical vein</a> becomes the <a href="/wiki/Ductus_venosus" title="Ductus venosus">ductus venosus</a> and the right <a href="/wiki/Vitelline_veins" title="Vitelline veins">vitelline vein</a> becomes the portal vein. The expanding liver bud is colonized by <a href="/wiki/Hematopoietic_stem_cell" title="Hematopoietic stem cell">hematopoietic cells</a>. The bipotential hepatoblasts begin differentiating into <a href="/wiki/Cholangiocyte" title="Cholangiocyte">biliary epithelial cells</a> and hepatocytes. The biliary epithelial cells differentiate from hepatoblasts around portal veins, first producing a monolayer, and then a bilayer of cuboidal cells. In ductal plate, focal dilations emerge at points in the bilayer, become surrounded by portal mesenchyme, and undergo tubulogenesis into intrahepatic bile ducts. Hepatoblasts not adjacent to portal veins instead differentiate into hepatocytes and arrange into cords lined by sinusoidal epithelial cells and bile canaliculi. Once hepatoblasts are specified into hepatocytes and undergo further expansion, they begin acquiring the functions of a mature hepatocyte, and eventually mature hepatocytes appear as highly polarized epithelial cells with abundant glycogen accumulation. In the adult liver, hepatocytes are not equivalent, with position along the portocentrovenular axis within a liver lobule dictating expression of metabolic genes involved in drug metabolism, carbohydrate metabolism, ammonia detoxification, and bile production and secretion. <a href="/wiki/Wnt_signaling_pathway" title="Wnt signaling pathway">WNT/β-catenin</a> has now been identified to be playing a key role in this phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21337461_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21337461-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ultrasound_liver_right_lobe_and_right_kidney.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Ultrasound_liver_right_lobe_and_right_kidney.jpg/220px-Ultrasound_liver_right_lobe_and_right_kidney.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Ultrasound_liver_right_lobe_and_right_kidney.jpg/330px-Ultrasound_liver_right_lobe_and_right_kidney.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Ultrasound_liver_right_lobe_and_right_kidney.jpg/440px-Ultrasound_liver_right_lobe_and_right_kidney.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ultrasound_liver_right_lobe_and_right_kidney.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult ultrasound showing the right lobe of the liver and right kidney.</div></div></div>
<p>At birth, the liver comprises roughly 4% of body weight and weighs on average about 120 g (4 oz). Over the course of further development, it will increase to 1.4–1.6 kg (3.1–3.5 lb) but will only take up 2.5–3.5% of body weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fetal_blood_supply">Fetal blood supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fetal blood supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the growing fetus, a major source of blood to the liver is the umbilical vein, which supplies nutrients to the growing fetus. The umbilical vein enters the abdomen at the umbilicus and passes upward along the free margin of the falciform ligament of the liver to the inferior surface of the liver. There, it joins with the left branch of the portal vein. The ductus venosus carries blood from the left portal vein to the left hepatic vein and then to the <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior vena cava</a>, allowing placental blood to bypass the liver. In the fetus, the liver does not perform the normal digestive processes and filtration of the infant liver because nutrients are received directly from the mother via the <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a>. The fetal liver releases some blood stem cells that migrate to the fetal <a href="/wiki/Thymus" title="Thymus">thymus</a>, creating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-cells" title="T-cells">T-cells</a> or T-lymphocytes. After birth, the formation of blood stem cells shifts to the red <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>. After 2–5 days, the umbilical vein and ductus venosus are obliterated; the former becomes the <a href="/wiki/Round_ligament_of_liver" title="Round ligament of liver">round ligament of liver</a> and the latter becomes the <a href="/wiki/Ligamentum_venosum" title="Ligamentum venosum">ligamentum venosum</a>.  In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_disorder" title="Medical disorder">disorders</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portal_hypertension" title="Portal hypertension">portal hypertension</a>, the umbilical vein can open up again.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Functions">Functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The various functions of the liver are carried out by the liver cells or hepatocytes. The liver is thought to be responsible for up to 500 separate functions, usually in combination with other systems and organs. Currently, no <a href="/wiki/Artificial_organ" title="Artificial organ">artificial organ</a> or device is capable of reproducing all the functions of the liver.  Some functions can be carried out by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_dialysis" title="Liver dialysis">liver dialysis</a>, an experimental treatment for <a href="/wiki/Liver_failure" title="Liver failure">liver failure</a>. The liver also accounts for about 20% of resting total body oxygen consumption.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_supply">Blood supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Blood supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The liver receives a dual blood supply from the hepatic portal vein and hepatic arteries. The hepatic portal vein delivers around 75% of the liver's blood supply and carries <a href="/wiki/Venous_blood" title="Venous blood">venous blood</a> drained from the spleen, gastrointestinal tract, and its associated organs. The hepatic arteries supply arterial blood to the liver, accounting for the remaining quarter of its blood flow. Oxygen is provided from both sources; about half of the liver's oxygen demand is met by the hepatic portal vein, and half is met by the hepatic arteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn1-55009-364-9_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn1-55009-364-9-39">[39]</a></sup> The hepatic artery also has both alpha- and beta-adrenergic receptors; therefore, flow through the artery is controlled, in part, by the splanchnic nerves of the autonomic nervous system.
</p><p>Blood flows through the liver sinusoids and empties into the central vein of each lobule.  The <a href="/wiki/Central_veins_of_liver" title="Central veins of liver">central veins</a> coalesce into hepatic veins, which leave the liver and drain into the inferior vena cava.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Human-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biliary_flow">Biliary flow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Biliary flow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Enterohepatic_circulation" title="Enterohepatic circulation">Enterohepatic circulation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_showing_the_position_of_the_perihilar_bile_ducts_CRUK_357.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="296" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Diagram_showing_the_position_of_the_perihilar_bile_ducts_CRUK_357.svg/220px-Diagram_showing_the_position_of_the_perihilar_bile_ducts_CRUK_357.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Diagram_showing_the_position_of_the_perihilar_bile_ducts_CRUK_357.svg/330px-Diagram_showing_the_position_of_the_perihilar_bile_ducts_CRUK_357.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Diagram_showing_the_position_of_the_perihilar_bile_ducts_CRUK_357.svg/440px-Diagram_showing_the_position_of_the_perihilar_bile_ducts_CRUK_357.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_showing_the_position_of_the_perihilar_bile_ducts_CRUK_357.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Biliary tract</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Biliary_tract" title="Biliary tract">biliary tract</a> is derived from the branches of the bile ducts. The biliary tract, also known as the biliary tree, is the path by which bile is secreted by the liver then transported to the first part of the small intestine, the <a href="/wiki/Duodenum" title="Duodenum">duodenum</a>. The bile produced in the liver is collected in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bile_canaliculi" title="Bile canaliculi">bile canaliculi</a>, small grooves between the faces of adjacent hepatocytes. The canaliculi radiate to the edge of the liver lobule, where they merge to form bile ducts. Within the liver, these ducts are termed intrahepatic bile ducts, and once they exit the liver, they are considered extrahepatic. The intrahepatic ducts eventually drain into the right and left hepatic ducts, which exit the liver at the <a href="/wiki/Porta_hepatis" title="Porta hepatis">transverse fissure</a>, and merge to form the common hepatic duct. The <a href="/wiki/Cystic_duct" title="Cystic duct">cystic duct</a> from the gallbladder joins with the common hepatic duct to form the common bile duct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Human-40">[40]</a></sup> The biliary system and connective tissue is supplied by the hepatic artery alone.
</p><p>Bile either drains directly into the duodenum via the common bile duct, or is temporarily stored in the gallbladder via the cystic duct. The common bile duct and the pancreatic duct enter the second part of the duodenum together at the hepatopancreatic ampulla, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Ampulla_of_Vater" title="Ampulla of Vater">ampulla of Vater</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolism">Metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Proteins_produced_and_secreted_by_the_liver" title="Proteins produced and secreted by the liver">Proteins produced and secreted by the liver</a></div>
<p>The liver plays a major role in carbohydrate, protein, amino acid, and lipid metabolism.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrate_metabolism">Carbohydrate metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Carbohydrate metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The liver performs several roles in <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate_metabolism" title="Carbohydrate metabolism">carbohydrate metabolism</a>: 
</p>
<ul><li>The liver synthesizes and stores around 100g of glycogen via <a href="/wiki/Glycogenesis" title="Glycogenesis">glycogenesis</a>, the formation of glycogen from <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>.</li>
<li>When needed, the liver releases glucose into the blood by performing <a href="/wiki/Glycogenolysis" title="Glycogenolysis">glycogenolysis</a>, the breakdown of glycogen into glucose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phys_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phys-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>The liver is also responsible for <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a>, which is the synthesis of glucose from certain <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>. Adipose and liver cells produce glycerol by <a href="/wiki/Lipolysis" title="Lipolysis">breakdown of fat</a>, which the liver uses for gluconeogenesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phys_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phys-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>Liver also does <b>glyconeogenesis</b> which is synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> from <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protein_metabolism">Protein metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Protein metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The liver is responsible for the mainstay of protein <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, synthesis as well as degradation. All <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_proteins" title="Plasma proteins">plasma proteins</a> except <a href="/wiki/Gamma_globulin" title="Gamma globulin">Gamma-globulins</a> are synthesised in the liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> It is also responsible for a large part of <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid_synthesis" title="Amino acid synthesis">amino acid synthesis</a>. The liver plays a role in the production of clotting factors, as well as red blood cell production. Some of the proteins synthesized by the liver include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coagulation_factors" title="Coagulation factors">coagulation factors</a> <a href="/wiki/Fibrinogen" title="Fibrinogen">I</a> (fibrinogen), <a href="/wiki/Thrombin" title="Thrombin">II</a> (prothrombin), <a href="/wiki/Factor_V" title="Factor V">V</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Factor_VII" title="Factor VII">VII</a>, <a href="/wiki/Factor_VIII" title="Factor VIII">VIII</a>, <a href="/wiki/Factor_IX" title="Factor IX">IX</a>, <a href="/wiki/Factor_X" title="Factor X">X</a>, <a href="/wiki/Factor_XI" title="Factor XI">XI</a>, <a href="/wiki/Factor_XII" title="Factor XII">XII</a>, <a href="/wiki/Factor_XIII" title="Factor XIII">XIII</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Protein_C" title="Protein C">protein C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein_S" title="Protein S">protein S</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antithrombin" title="Antithrombin">antithrombin</a>. The liver is a major site of production for <a href="/wiki/Thrombopoietin" title="Thrombopoietin">thrombopoietin</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Glycoprotein" title="Glycoprotein">glycoprotein</a> hormone that regulates the production of <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelets</a> by the bone marrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lipid_metabolism">Lipid metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Lipid metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The liver plays several roles in <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> metabolism: it performs <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> synthesis, <a href="/wiki/Lipogenesis" title="Lipogenesis">lipogenesis</a>, and the production of <a href="/wiki/Triglyceride" title="Triglyceride">triglycerides</a>, and a bulk of the body's lipoproteins are synthesized in the liver. The liver plays a key role in digestion, as it produces and excretes <a href="/wiki/Bile" title="Bile">bile</a> (a yellowish liquid) required for emulsifying fats and help the absorption of vitamin K from the diet. Some of the bile drains directly into the duodenum, and some is stored in the gallbladder. The liver produces <a href="/wiki/Insulin-like_growth_factor_1" title="Insulin-like growth factor 1">insulin-like growth factor 1</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polypeptide" title="Polypeptide">polypeptide</a> <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> hormone that plays an important role in childhood growth and continues to have <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolic effects</a> in adults.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breakdown">Breakdown</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Breakdown">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The liver is responsible for the breakdown of <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>. The liver breaks down <a href="/wiki/Bilirubin" title="Bilirubin">bilirubin</a> via <a href="/wiki/Glucuronidation" title="Glucuronidation">glucuronidation</a>, facilitating its excretion into bile.
The liver is responsible for the breakdown and excretion of many waste products. It plays a key role in breaking down or modifying toxic substances (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Methylation" title="Methylation">methylation</a>) and most medicinal products in a process called <a href="/wiki/Drug_metabolism" title="Drug metabolism">drug metabolism</a>. This sometimes results in <a href="/wiki/Toxication" title="Toxication">toxication</a>, when the metabolite is more toxic than its precursor. Preferably, the toxins are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenobiotic_conjugation" title="Xenobiotic conjugation">conjugated</a> to avail excretion in bile or urine. The liver converts <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> into <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">ornithine cycle</a> or the urea cycle, and the urea is excreted in the urine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anatomy_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anatomy-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_reservoir">Blood reservoir</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Blood reservoir">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because the liver is an expandable organ, large quantities of
blood can be stored in its blood vessels. Its normal blood
volume, including both that in the hepatic veins and that in
the hepatic sinuses, is about 450 milliliters, or almost 10 percent of the body's total blood volume. When high pressure
in the right atrium causes backpressure in the liver, the liver
expands, and 0.5 to 1 liter of extra blood is occasionally stored
in the hepatic veins and sinuses. This occurs especially in cardiac failure with peripheral congestion. Thus, in effect, the liver is a large, expandable, venous organ capable of acting as a valuable blood reservoir in times of excess blood volume and capable of supplying extra blood in times of diminished blood volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lymph_production">Lymph production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Lymph production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because the pores in the hepatic sinusoids are very permeable and allow ready passage of both fluid and proteins into the <a href="/wiki/Perisinusoidal_space" title="Perisinusoidal space">perisinusoidal space</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lymph" title="Lymph">lymph</a> draining from the liver usually has a protein concentration of about 6 g/dl, which is only slightly less than the protein concentration of plasma. 
Also, the high permeability of the liver sinusoid epithelium allows large quantities of lymph to form. Therefore, about half of all the lymph formed in the body under resting conditions arises in the liver.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>The liver stores a multitude of substances, including <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a> (1–2 years' supply), <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a> (1–4 months' supply),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B<sub>12</sub></a> (3–5 years' supply),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">vitamin K</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_E" title="Vitamin E">vitamin E</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, etc.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Haematopoiesis" title="Haematopoiesis">Haemopoiesis</a> - The formation of blood cells is called haemopoiesis. In embryonic stage RBC and WBC are formed by liver. In the first trimester fetus, the liver is the main site of <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cell</a> production. By the 32nd week of <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a> has almost completely taken over that task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li>Liver helps in purification of blood. The <a href="/wiki/Kupffer_cell" title="Kupffer cell">Kupffer cells</a> of liver are <a href="/wiki/Phagocyte" title="Phagocyte">phagocytic cells</a>, helps in phagocytosis of dead blood cells and bacteria from the blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></li>
<li>The liver is responsible for immunological effects – the <a href="/wiki/Mononuclear_phagocyte_system" title="Mononuclear phagocyte system">mononuclear phagocyte system</a> of the liver contains many immunologically active cells, acting as a 'sieve' for antigens carried to it via the <a href="/wiki/Hepatic_portal_system" title="Hepatic portal system">portal system</a>.</li>
<li>The liver produces <a href="/wiki/Albumin" title="Albumin">albumin</a>, the most abundant protein in <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">blood serum</a>. It is essential in the maintenance of <a href="/wiki/Oncotic_pressure" title="Oncotic pressure">oncotic pressure</a>, and acts as a transport for <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steroid_hormone" title="Steroid hormone">steroid hormones</a>.</li>
<li>The liver synthesizes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiotensinogen" title="Angiotensinogen">angiotensinogen</a>, a hormone that is responsible for raising the <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> when activated by <a href="/wiki/Renin" title="Renin">renin</a>, an enzyme that is released when the <a href="/wiki/Juxtaglomerular_apparatus" title="Juxtaglomerular apparatus">kidney</a> senses <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">low blood pressure</a>.</li>
<li>The liver produces the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Catalase" title="Catalase">catalase</a> to break down <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>, a toxic <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidising agent</a>, into water and oxygen.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="With_aging">With aging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: With aging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The oxidative capacity of the liver decreases with aging, and therefore any medications that require oxidation (for instance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzodiazepines" title="Benzodiazepines">benzodiazepines</a>) are more likely to accumulate to toxic levels. However, medications with shorter <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Lorazepam" title="Lorazepam">lorazepam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxazepam" title="Oxazepam">oxazepam</a>, are preferred in most cases when benzodiazepines are required in regard to <a href="/wiki/Geriatrics" title="Geriatrics">geriatric medicine</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_significance">Clinical significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Clinical significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disease">Disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liver_disease" title="Liver disease">Liver disease</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Big_Liver_Tumor.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Big_Liver_Tumor.JPG/220px-Big_Liver_Tumor.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Big_Liver_Tumor.JPG/330px-Big_Liver_Tumor.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Big_Liver_Tumor.JPG/440px-Big_Liver_Tumor.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Big_Liver_Tumor.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Left lobe liver tumor</div></div></div>
<p>The liver is a vital organ and supports almost every other organ in the body. Because of its strategic location and multidimensional functions, the liver is prone to many diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The bare area of the liver is a site that is vulnerable to the passing of infection from the abdominal cavity to the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_cavity" title="Thoracic cavity">thoracic cavity</a>. Liver diseases may be diagnosed by <a href="/wiki/Liver_function_tests" title="Liver function tests">liver function tests</a>–blood tests that can identify various markers. For example, <a href="/wiki/Acute-phase_protein" title="Acute-phase protein">acute-phase reactants</a> are produced by the liver in response to injury or inflammation.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hepatitis" title="Hepatitis">Hepatitis</a> is a common condition of inflammation of the liver. The most usual cause of this is <a href="/wiki/Viral_hepatitis" title="Viral hepatitis">viral</a>, and the most common of these infections are <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">hepatitis A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C" title="Hepatitis C">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_D" title="Hepatitis D">D</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_E" title="Hepatitis E">E</a>. Some of these infections are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_diseases" title="Sexually transmitted diseases">sexually transmitted</a>. Inflammation can also be caused by other viruses in the family <a href="/wiki/Herpesviridae" title="Herpesviridae">Herpesviridae</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Herpes_simplex_virus" title="Herpes simplex virus">herpes simplex virus</a></i>.  Chronic (rather than acute) infection with hepatitis B virus or hepatitis C virus is the main cause of <a href="/wiki/Liver_cancer" title="Liver cancer">liver cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>  Globally, about 248 million individuals are chronically infected with hepatitis B (with 843,724 in the U.S.),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26231459_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26231459-53">[53]</a></sup> and 142 million are chronically infected with hepatitis C<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Global-54">[54]</a></sup> (with 2.7 million in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlwww.hepatitisc.uw.edu_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlwww.hepatitisc.uw.edu-55">[55]</a></sup>).  Globally there are about 114 million and 20 million cases of hepatitis A<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Global-54">[54]</a></sup> and hepatitis E<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlWHO_|_Hepatitis_E_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlWHO_|_Hepatitis_E-56">[56]</a></sup> respectively, but these generally resolve and do not become chronic.  Hepatitis D virus is a "satellite" of hepatitis B virus (it can only infect in the presence of hepatitis B), and co-infects nearly 20 million people with hepatitis B, globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16903225_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16903225-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hepatic_encephalopathy" title="Hepatic encephalopathy">Hepatic encephalopathy</a> is caused by an accumulation of toxins in the bloodstream that are normally removed by the liver. This condition can result in coma and can prove fatal. <a href="/wiki/Budd%E2%80%93Chiari_syndrome" title="Budd–Chiari syndrome">Budd–Chiari syndrome</a> is a condition caused by <a href="/wiki/Vascular_occlusion" title="Vascular occlusion">blockage</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hepatic_veins" title="Hepatic veins">hepatic veins</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Thrombosis" title="Thrombosis">thrombosis</a>) that drain the liver. It presents with the classical triad of abdominal pain, <a href="/wiki/Ascites" title="Ascites">ascites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hepatomegaly" title="Hepatomegaly">liver enlargement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Many diseases of the liver are accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Jaundice" title="Jaundice">jaundice</a> caused by increased levels of <a href="/wiki/Bilirubin" title="Bilirubin">bilirubin</a> in the system. The bilirubin results from the breakup of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemoglobin" title="Haemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> of dead <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>; normally, the liver removes bilirubin from the blood and excretes it through bile.
</p><p>Other disorders caused by excessive alcohol consumption are grouped under <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_liver_disease" title="Alcoholic liver disease">alcoholic liver diseases</a> and these include <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_hepatitis" title="Alcoholic hepatitis">alcoholic hepatitis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatty_liver" title="Fatty liver">fatty liver</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>. Factors contributing to the development of alcoholic liver diseases are not only the quantity and frequency of alcohol consumption, but can also include gender, genetics, and liver insult. Liver damage can also be <a href="/wiki/Hepatotoxicity" title="Hepatotoxicity">caused by drugs</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Paracetamol" title="Paracetamol">paracetamol</a> and drugs used to treat cancer. A rupture of the liver can be caused by a <a href="/wiki/Liver_shot" title="Liver shot">liver shot</a> used in combat sports.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Primary_biliary_cholangitis" title="Primary biliary cholangitis">Primary biliary cholangitis</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_disease" title="Autoimmune disease">autoimmune disease</a> of the liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dancygier2010_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dancygier2010-60">[60]</a></sup> It is marked by slow progressive destruction of the small <a href="/wiki/Bile_duct" title="Bile duct">bile ducts</a> of the liver, with the intralobular ducts (<a href="/wiki/Canals_of_Hering" title="Canals of Hering">Canals of Hering</a>) affected early in the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> When these ducts are damaged, <a href="/wiki/Bile" title="Bile">bile</a> and other toxins build up in the liver (<a href="/wiki/Cholestasis" title="Cholestasis">cholestasis</a>) and over time damages the liver tissue in combination with ongoing immune related damage. This can lead to scarring (<a href="/wiki/Fibrosis" title="Fibrosis">fibrosis</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>. Cirrhosis increases the resistance to blood flow in the liver, and can result in <a href="/wiki/Portal_hypertension" title="Portal hypertension">portal hypertension</a>. <a href="/wiki/Portacaval_anastomosis" title="Portacaval anastomosis">Congested anastomoses</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Portal_venous_system" title="Portal venous system">portal venous system</a> and the systemic circulation, can be a subsequent condition.
</p><p>There are also many pediatric liver diseases, including <a href="/wiki/Biliary_atresia" title="Biliary atresia">biliary atresia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpha-1_antitrypsin_deficiency" title="Alpha-1 antitrypsin deficiency">alpha-1 antitrypsin deficiency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alagille_syndrome" title="Alagille syndrome">alagille syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Progressive_familial_intrahepatic_cholestasis" title="Progressive familial intrahepatic cholestasis">progressive familial intrahepatic cholestasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Langerhans_cell_histiocytosis" title="Langerhans cell histiocytosis">Langerhans cell histiocytosis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cavernous_liver_haemangioma" title="Cavernous liver haemangioma">hepatic hemangioma</a> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benign_tumour" title="Benign tumour">benign tumour</a> the most common type of liver tumour, thought to be congenital. A genetic disorder causing multiple cysts to form in the liver tissue, usually in later life, and usually asymptomatic, is <a href="/wiki/Polycystic_liver_disease" title="Polycystic liver disease">polycystic liver disease</a>. Diseases that interfere with liver function will lead to derangement of these processes. However, the liver has a great capacity to <a href="/wiki/Regeneration_(biology)" title="Regeneration (biology)">regenerate</a> and has a large reserve capacity. In most cases, the liver only produces symptoms after extensive damage.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hepatomegaly" title="Hepatomegaly">Hepatomegaly</a> refers to an enlarged liver and can be due to many causes. It can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palpated" title="Palpated">palpated</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Liver_span" title="Liver span">liver span</a> measurement.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symptoms">Symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The classic symptoms of liver damage include the following:
</p>
<ul><li>Pale stools occur when <a href="/wiki/Stercobilin" title="Stercobilin">stercobilin</a>, a brown pigment, is absent from the stool. Stercobilin is derived from bilirubin metabolites produced in the liver.</li>
<li>Dark urine occurs when bilirubin mixes with urine</li>
<li>Jaundice (yellow skin and/or whites of the eyes) This is where <a href="/wiki/Bilirubin" title="Bilirubin">bilirubin</a> deposits in skin, causing an intense <a href="/wiki/Itch" title="Itch">itch</a>. Itching is the most common complaint by people who have liver failure. Often this itch cannot be relieved by drugs.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ascites" title="Ascites">Swelling of the abdomen</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swelling_(medical)" title="Swelling (medical)">swelling</a> of the ankles and feet occurs because the <a href="/wiki/Hypoalbuminemia" title="Hypoalbuminemia">liver fails to make albumin</a>.</li>
<li>Excessive fatigue occurs from a <a href="/wiki/Deficiency_(medicine)" title="Deficiency (medicine)">generalized loss of nutrients</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_mineral" title="Dietary mineral">minerals</a> and vitamins.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bruise" title="Bruise">Bruising</a> and easy bleeding are other features of liver disease. The liver makes <a href="/wiki/Coagulation" title="Coagulation">clotting factors</a>, substances which help prevent bleeding. When liver damage occurs, these factors are no longer present and severe bleeding can occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li>Pain in the upper right quadrant can result from the stretching of Glisson's capsule in conditions of <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis" title="Hepatitis">hepatitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-eclampsia" title="Pre-eclampsia">pre-eclampsia</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The diagnosis of liver disease is made by <a href="/wiki/Liver_function_tests" title="Liver function tests">liver function tests</a>, groups of <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood tests</a>, that can readily show the extent of liver damage. If <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a> is suspected, then other <a href="/wiki/Serology" title="Serology">serological</a> tests will be carried out. A physical examination of the liver can only reveal its size and any tenderness, and some form of <a href="/wiki/Medical_imaging" title="Medical imaging">imaging</a> such as an <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a> or <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a> may also be needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
Sometimes a <a href="/wiki/Liver_biopsy" title="Liver biopsy">liver biopsy</a> will be necessary, and a tissue sample is taken through a needle inserted into the skin just below the <a href="/wiki/Rib_cage" title="Rib cage">rib cage</a>. This procedure may be helped by a sonographer providing ultrasound guidance to an interventional radiologist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
