<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liver_regeneration">Liver regeneration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Liver regeneration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liver_regeneration" title="Liver regeneration">Liver regeneration</a></div>
<p>The liver is the only human internal organ capable of natural <a href="/wiki/Regeneration_(biology)" title="Regeneration (biology)">regeneration</a> of lost <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_tissue" title="Biological tissue">tissue</a>; as little as 25% of a liver can regenerate into a whole liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-regen_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-regen-66">[66]</a></sup>  This is, however, not true regeneration but rather <a href="/wiki/Compensatory_growth_(organ)" title="Compensatory growth (organ)">compensatory growth</a> in mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>  The lobes that are removed do not regrow and the growth of the liver is a restoration of function, not original form.  This contrasts with true regeneration where both original function and form are restored. In some other species, such as zebrafish, the liver undergoes true regeneration by restoring both shape and size of the organ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In the liver, large areas of the tissues are formed but for the formation of new cells there must be sufficient amount of material so the circulation of the blood becomes more active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>This is predominantly due to the <a href="/wiki/Hepatocyte" title="Hepatocyte">hepatocytes</a> re-entering the <a href="/wiki/Cell_cycle" title="Cell cycle">cell cycle</a>. That is, the hepatocytes go from the quiescent <a href="/wiki/G0_phase" title="G0 phase">G0 phase</a> to the <a href="/wiki/G1_phase" title="G1 phase">G1 phase</a> and undergo mitosis. This process is activated by the <a href="/wiki/Low-affinity_nerve_growth_factor_receptor" title="Low-affinity nerve growth factor receptor">p75</a> receptors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18515089_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18515089-70">[70]</a></sup> There is also some evidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipotential" title="Bipotential">bipotential</a> <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cells</a>, called hepatic oval cells or ovalocytes (not to be confused with oval red blood cells of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian_ovalocytosis" title="Southeast Asian ovalocytosis">ovalocytosis</a>), which are thought to reside in the <a href="/wiki/Canals_of_Hering" title="Canals of Hering">canals of Hering</a>. These cells can differentiate into either <a href="/wiki/Hepatocyte" title="Hepatocyte">hepatocytes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cholangiocyte" title="Cholangiocyte">cholangiocytes</a>. Cholangiocytes are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epithelial_cells" title="Epithelial cells">epithelial lining cells</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bile_duct" title="Bile duct">bile ducts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16550043_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16550043-71">[71]</a></sup> They are cuboidal epithelium in the small interlobular bile ducts, but become columnar and mucus secreting in larger bile ducts approaching the porta hepatis and the extrahepatic ducts. Research is being carried out on the use of stem cells for the generation of an <a href="/wiki/Artificial_organ" title="Artificial organ">artificial liver</a>.
</p><p>Scientific and medical works about liver regeneration often refer to the Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titan</a> <a href="/wiki/Prometheus" title="Prometheus">Prometheus</a> who was chained to a rock in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> where, each day, his liver was devoured by an eagle, only to grow back each night. The myth suggests the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a> may have known about the liver's remarkable capacity for self-repair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liver_transplantation">Liver transplantation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Liver transplantation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liver_transplantation" title="Liver transplantation">Liver transplantation</a></div>
<p>Human liver transplants were first performed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Starzl" title="Thomas Starzl">Thomas Starzl</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roy_Calne" title="Roy Calne">Roy Calne</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> in 1963 and 1967, respectively.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liver..JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Liver..JPG/220px-Liver..JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Liver..JPG/330px-Liver..JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Liver..JPG/440px-Liver..JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liver..JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After resection of left lobe liver tumor</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Liver_transplantation" title="Liver transplantation">Liver transplantation</a> is the only option for those with irreversible liver failure. Most transplants are done for chronic liver diseases leading to <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>, such as chronic <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C" title="Hepatitis C">hepatitis C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>, and autoimmune hepatitis. Less commonly, liver transplantation is done for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulminant_hepatic_failure" title="Fulminant hepatic failure">fulminant hepatic failure</a>, in which liver failure occurs rapidly over a period of days or weeks.
</p><p>Liver <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allograft" title="Allograft">allografts</a> for <a href="/wiki/Organ_transplantation" title="Organ transplantation">transplant</a> usually come from donors who have died from fatal <a href="/wiki/Traumatic_brain_injury" title="Traumatic brain injury">brain injury</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Living_donor_liver_transplantation" title="Living donor liver transplantation">Living donor liver transplantation</a> is a technique in which a portion of a living person's liver is removed (<a href="/wiki/Hepatectomy" title="Hepatectomy">hepatectomy</a>) and used to replace the entire liver of the recipient.  This was first performed in 1989 for pediatric liver transplantation.  Only 20 percent of an adult's liver (Couinaud segments 2 and 3) is needed to serve as a liver allograft for an infant or small child.
</p><p>More recently,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2018)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> adult-to-adult liver transplantation has been done using the donor's right hepatic lobe, which amounts to 60 percent of the liver.  Due to the ability of the liver to <a href="/wiki/Regeneration_(biology)" title="Regeneration (biology)">regenerate</a>, both the donor and recipient end up with normal liver function if all goes well. This procedure is more controversial, as it entails performing a much larger operation on the donor, and indeed there were at least two donor deaths out of the first several hundred cases. A 2006 publication addressed the problem of donor mortality and found at least fourteen cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The risk of postoperative complications (and death) is far greater in right-sided operations than that in left-sided operations.
</p><p>With the recent advances of noninvasive imaging, living liver donors usually have to undergo imaging examinations for liver anatomy to decide if the anatomy is feasible for donation. The evaluation is usually performed by multidetector row <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computed_tomography" title="Computed tomography">computed tomography</a> (MDCT) and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">magnetic resonance imaging</a> (MRI). MDCT is good in vascular anatomy and volumetry. MRI is used for biliary tree anatomy. Donors with very unusual vascular anatomy, which makes them unsuitable for donation, could be screened out to avoid unnecessary operations.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">

</p>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some cultures regard the liver as the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, the gods punished <a href="/wiki/Prometheus" title="Prometheus">Prometheus</a> for revealing fire to humans by chaining him to a rock where a <a href="/wiki/Vulture" title="Vulture">vulture</a> (or an <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagle</a>) would peck out his liver, which would regenerate overnight. (The liver is the only human internal organ that actually can regenerate itself to a significant extent.) Many ancient peoples of the Near East and Mediterranean areas practiced a type of <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haruspicy" title="Haruspicy">haruspicy</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatomancy" title="Hepatomancy">hepatomancy</a>, where they tried to obtain information by examining the livers of sheep and other animals.
</p><p>In Plato, and in later physiology, the liver was thought to be the seat of the darkest emotions (specifically wrath, jealousy and greed) which drive men to action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a> (tractate <i>Berakhot 61b</i>) refers to the liver as the seat of <a href="/wiki/Anger" title="Anger">anger</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder" title="Gallbladder">gallbladder</a> counteracting this. The <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> languages (<span title="Urdu-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ur">جگر</span></span> or <span title="Hindi-language text"><span lang="hi">जिगर</span></span> or <span title="Hindi-language romanization"><i lang="hi-Latn">jigar</i></span>) refer to the liver in figurative speech to indicate courage and strong feelings, or "their best"; e.g., "This <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> has thrown to you the pieces of its liver!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The term <span title="Urdu-language romanization"><i lang="ur-Latn">jan e jigar</i></span>, literally "the strength (power) of my liver", is a term of endearment in Urdu. In Persian slang, <span title="Persian-language romanization"><i lang="fa-Latn">jigar</i></span> is used as an adjective for any object which is desirable, especially women. In the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_language" title="Zulu language">Zulu language</a>, the word for liver (<span title="Zulu-language text"><i lang="zu">isibindi</i></span>) is the same as the word for courage. In English the term 'lily-livered' is used to indicate cowardice from the medieval belief that the liver was the seat of courage.
Spanish <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">hígados</i></span> also means "courage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DRAE_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-DRAE-77">[77]</a></sup>
However the secondary meaning of Basque <span title="Basque-language text"><i lang="eu">gibel</i></span> is "indolence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Azkue_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Azkue-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>In biblical Hebrew, the word for liver, <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="hbo">כבד</span></span> (<span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">Kauved</i></span>, stemmed <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">KBD</i></span> or <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">KVD</i></span>, similar to Arabic <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">الكبد</span></span>), also means <b>heavy</b> and is used to describe <b>the rich</b> ("heavy"  with possessions) and <b>honor</b> (presumably for the same reason). In the Book of Lamentations (2:11) it is used to describe the physiological responses to sadness by "my liver spilled to earth" along with the flow of tears and the overturning in bitterness of the intestines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> On several occasions in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_Psalms" title="Book of Psalms">book of Psalms</a> (most notably <a href="/wiki/Psalm_16" title="Psalm 16">16</a>:9), the word is used to describe happiness in the liver, along with the heart (which beats rapidly) and the flesh (which appears red under the skin).  Further usage as <b>the self</b> (similar to "your honor") is widely available throughout the old testament, sometimes compared to the breathing soul (Genesis 49:6, Psalms 7:6, etc.). An honorable hat was also referred to with this word (Job 19:9, etc.) and under that definition appears many times along with <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="hbo">פאר</span></span> <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">Pe'er</i></span> - grandeur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>These four meanings were used in preceding ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Asiatic_languages" title="Afro-Asiatic languages">Afro-Asiatic languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> and Ancient Egyptian preserved in classical Ethiopic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liver_(food)" title="Liver (food)">Liver (food)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Finnish_maksalaatikko.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Finnish_maksalaatikko.JPG/220px-Finnish_maksalaatikko.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Finnish_maksalaatikko.JPG/330px-Finnish_maksalaatikko.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Finnish_maksalaatikko.JPG/440px-Finnish_maksalaatikko.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Finnish_maksalaatikko.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Maksalaatikko" title="Maksalaatikko">Maksalaatikko</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finnish</a> liver casserole</div></div></div>
<p>Humans commonly eat the livers of mammals, fowl, and fish as food. Domestic pig, ox, lamb, calf, chicken, and goose livers are widely available from butchers and supermarkets. In the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>, the anatomical word for "liver" (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i>foie</i>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> <i>hígado</i>, etc.) derives not from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> anatomical term, <i>jecur</i>, but from the culinary term <i>ficatum</i>, literally "stuffed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Figs" title="Figs">figs</a>," referring to the livers of geese that had been fattened on figs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Animal livers are rich in iron, <a href="/wiki/Retinol" title="Retinol">vitamin A</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B<sub>12</sub></a>; and <a href="/wiki/Cod_liver_oil" title="Cod liver oil">cod liver oil</a> is commonly used as a <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplement</a>.
</p><p>Liver can be baked, boiled, broiled, fried, stir-fried, or eaten raw (<i>asbeh nayeh</i> or <i>sawda naye</i> in <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_cuisine" title="Lebanese cuisine">Lebanese cuisine</a>, or liver <a href="/wiki/Sashimi" title="Sashimi">sashimi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese cuisine</a>). In many preparations, pieces of liver are combined with pieces of meat or kidneys, as in the various forms of Middle Eastern mixed grill (e.g. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meurav_Yerushalmi" title="Meurav Yerushalmi">meurav Yerushalmi</a></i>). Well-known examples include <a href="/wiki/Liver_p%C3%A2t%C3%A9" title="Liver pâté">liver pâté</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foie_gras" title="Foie gras">foie gras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chopped_liver" title="Chopped liver">chopped liver</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leverpastej" title="Leverpastej">leverpastej</a>. Liver <a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Braunschweiger_(sausage)" title="Braunschweiger (sausage)">Braunschweiger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liverwurst" title="Liverwurst">liverwurst</a>, are also a valued meal. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_sausage" title="Liver sausage">Liver sausages</a> may also be used as spreads. A traditional <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South African</a> delicacy, <a href="/wiki/Skilpadjies" title="Skilpadjies">skilpadjies</a>, is made of minced lamb's liver wrapped in <i>netvet</i> (caul fat), and grilled over an open fire. Traditionally, some fish livers were valued as food, especially the <a href="/wiki/Common_stingray" title="Common stingray">stingray</a> liver. It was used to prepare delicacies, such as poached skate liver on toast in England, as well as the <i>beignets de foie de raie</i> and <i>foie de raie en croute</i> in <a href="/wiki/French_cuisine" title="French cuisine">French cuisine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Giraffe_liver">Giraffe liver</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Giraffe liver">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ESCAYRAC(1853)_p454_Planche_VIII.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1561" data-file-width="2497" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ESCAYRAC%281853%29_p454_Planche_VIII.jpg/220px-ESCAYRAC%281853%29_p454_Planche_VIII.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ESCAYRAC%281853%29_p454_Planche_VIII.jpg/330px-ESCAYRAC%281853%29_p454_Planche_VIII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ESCAYRAC%281853%29_p454_Planche_VIII.jpg/440px-ESCAYRAC%281853%29_p454_Planche_VIII.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ESCAYRAC(1853)_p454_Planche_VIII.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>19th century drinking scene in <a href="/wiki/Kordofan" title="Kordofan">Kordofan</a>, home to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humur" title="Humur">Humr tribe</a>, who made a drink from giraffe liver. Plate from <i>Le Désert et le Soudan</i> by <a href="/wiki/Stanislas_d%27Escayrac_de_Lauture" title="Stanislas d'Escayrac de Lauture">Stanislas d'Escayrac de Lauture</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humur" title="Humur">Humr</a> are one of the tribes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baggara" title="Baggara">Baggara</a> ethnic group, native to southwestern <a href="/wiki/Kordofan" title="Kordofan">Kordofan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> who speak <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuwa_Arabic" title="Shuwa Arabic"><i>Shuwa</i></a> (<a href="/wiki/Chadian_Arabic" title="Chadian Arabic">Chadian Arabic</a>), make a non-alcoholic drink from the liver and <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Giraffe" title="Giraffe">giraffe</a>, which they call <i>umm nyolokh</i>. They claim it is intoxicating (Arabic  سكران  <i>sakran</i>), causing dreams and even waking <a href="/wiki/Hallucination" title="Hallucination">hallucinations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudgley_Psychoactive_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rudgley_Psychoactive-84">[84]</a></sup> Anthropologist Ian Cunnison accompanied the Humr on one of their giraffe-hunting expeditions in the late 1950s, and noted that:
</p>
<dl><dd>It is said that a person, once he has drunk <i>umm nyolokh</i>, will return to giraffe again and again. Humr, being <a href="/wiki/Ansar_(Sudan)" title="Ansar (Sudan)">Mahdists</a>, are strict <a href="/wiki/Khamr" title="Khamr">abstainers</a> [from alcohol] and a Humrawi is never drunk (<i>sakran</i>) on <a href="/wiki/Liquor" title="Liquor">liquor</a> or beer. But he uses this word to describe the effects which <i>umm nyolokh</i> has upon him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunnison_Giraffe_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunnison_Giraffe-85">[85]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Cunnison's remarkable account of an apparently psychoactive mammal found its way from a somewhat obscure scientific paper into more mainstream literature through a conversation between <a href="/wiki/Wendy_James_(anthropologist)" title="Wendy James (anthropologist)">W. James</a> of the Institute of Social and Cultural <a href="/wiki/Social_anthropology" title="Social anthropology">Anthropology</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> and specialist on the use of <a href="/wiki/Hallucinogen" title="Hallucinogen">hallucinogens</a> and intoxicants in society, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rudgley" title="Richard Rudgley">R. Rudgley</a>, who discussed it in a book on psychoactive drugs for general readers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudgley_Psychoactive_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rudgley_Psychoactive-84">[84]</a></sup> He speculated that a hallucinogenic compound <a href="/wiki/N,N-Dimethyltryptamine" title="N,N-Dimethyltryptamine">N,N-Dimethyltryptamine</a> in the giraffe liver might account for the intoxicating properties claimed for <i>umm nyolokh</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudgley_Psychoactive_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rudgley_Psychoactive-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cunnison, on the other hand, writing in 1958 found it hard to believe in the literal truth of the Humr's assertion that the drink was intoxicating:
</p>
<dl><dd>I can only assume that there is no intoxicating substance in the drink, and that the effect it produces is simply a matter of convention, although it may be brought about <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">subconsciously</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunnison_Giraffe_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cunnison_Giraffe-85">[85]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>The study of <a href="/wiki/Entheogen" title="Entheogen">entheogens</a> in general – including entheogens of animal origin (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Hallucinogenic_fish" title="Hallucinogenic fish">hallucinogenic fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colorado_River_toad" title="Colorado River toad">toad venom</a>) – has, however, made considerable progress in the sixty-odd years since Cunnison's report; the idea that some intoxicating substance might reside in giraffe livers may no longer be as far-fetched as it seemed to Cunnison. However, to date, proof (or disproof) still waits on detailed analyses of the organ and the beverage made from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudgley_Psychoactive_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rudgley_Psychoactive-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Arrow.2Fbullet_poison"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrow/bullet_poison">Arrow/bullet poison</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Arrow/bullet poison">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Certain <a href="/wiki/Tungusic_peoples" title="Tungusic peoples">Tungusic peoples</a> of northeast Asia formerly prepared a type of <a href="/wiki/Arrow_poison" title="Arrow poison">arrow poison</a> from rotting animal livers, which was, in later times, also applied to <a href="/wiki/Bullet" title="Bullet">bullets</a>. Russian anthropologist <a href="/wiki/S._M._Shirokogoroff" title="S. M. Shirokogoroff">S. M. Shirokogoroff</a> wrote that:
</p>
<dl><dd>Formerly the using of poisoned arrows was common. For instance, among the Kumarčen, [a subgroup of the <a href="/wiki/Oroqen_people" title="Oroqen people">Oroqen</a>] even in recent times, a poison was used which was prepared from decaying liver.</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>[Note] This has been confirmed by the Kumarčen. I am not competent to judge as to the chemical conditions of production of poison which is not destroyed by the heat of explosion. However, the Tungus themselves compare this method [of poisoning ammunition] with the poisoning of arrows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Other animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leber_Schaf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1210" data-file-width="1703" decoding="async" height="92" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Leber_Schaf.jpg/130px-Leber_Schaf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Leber_Schaf.jpg/195px-Leber_Schaf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Leber_Schaf.jpg/260px-Leber_Schaf.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leber_Schaf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">Sheep</a>'s liver</div></div></div>
<p>The liver is found in all <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> and is typically the largest internal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organ</a>. The internal structure of the liver is broadly similar in all vertebrates, though its form varies considerably in different species, and is largely determined by the shape and arrangement of the surrounding organs. Nonetheless, in most species it is divided into right and left lobes; exceptions to this general rule include <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, where the shape of the body necessitates a simple cigar-like form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-VB-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>An organ sometimes referred to as a liver is found associated with the digestive tract of the primitive chordate <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphioxus" title="Amphioxus">Amphioxus</a></i>. Although it performs many functions of a liver, it is not considered a "true" liver but rather a <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)"><i>homolog</i></a> of the vertebrate liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YuanRuan2015_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-YuanRuan2015-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YuLecroisey2015_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-YuLecroisey2015-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EscrivaChao2012_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-EscrivaChao2012-90">[90]</a></sup> The amphioxus hepatic caecum produces the liver-specific proteins <a href="/wiki/Vitellogenin" title="Vitellogenin">vitellogenin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antithrombin" title="Antithrombin">antithrombin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plasmin" title="Plasmin">plasminogen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alanine_aminotransferase" title="Alanine aminotransferase">alanine aminotransferase</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>/<a href="/wiki/Insulin-like_growth_factor" title="Insulin-like growth factor">Insulin-like growth factor</a> (IGF)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuoZhang2009_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuoZhang2009-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An argument for the ancient Greek’s knowing about liver regeneration is provided by <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFChenChen1994">Chen, T.S.; Chen, P.S. <li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span title="Biblical Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="hbo">כלו בדמעות עיני חמרמרו מעי נשפך לארץ כבדי על שבר בת עמי בעטף עולל ויונק ברחבות קריה</span></span> "My eyes terminated with tears, my intestines overturned with bitterness, my liver spilled to the earth on the breaking of my nation's daughter when a suckling baby lies in the town squares" (Lamentations 2:11) this could be interpreted to be read as my <b>honor</b> spilled, or <b>myself</b> being spilled).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://www.ruvik.co.il/סיפורי-מילים/כ/כבוד.aspx" rel="nofollow">Kavod - Honor</a> (in Hebrew, Israeli Linguist Ruvik Rosenthal's website). Rosenthal hypothesized that the term's usage to describe heaviness comes perhaps from the liver being the heaviest of all body parts in some farm animals or in humans.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See 
<a class="external text" href="https://www.assyrianlanguages.org/akkadian/dosearch.php?searchkey=kab%C4%81tu&amp;language=rawakkadian" rel="nofollow">Kabadu</a> in Akkadian, (From online dictionary at Association Assyrophile de France organization website)</span>
</li>
